    function loadScript(url, callback) {
      var script = document.createElement("script");
      script.type = "text/javascript";
    
      if (script.readyState) {  //Internet Explorer
          script.onreadystatechange = function() {
            if (script.readyState == "loaded" ||
                    script.readyState == "complete") {
              script.onreadystatechange = null;
              callback();
            }
          };
      } else {  //Other browsers
        script.onload = function() {
          callback();
        };
      }
    
      script.src = url;
      document.getElementsByTagName("head")[0].appendChild(script);
    }

      AppleID.auth.init({
        clientId : 'com.goodreads.app', 
        scope : 'name email',

<span id="freeTextContainer10379968742217453048">Paula Spencer is a thirty-nine-year-old working-class woman struggling to reclaim her dignity after marriage to an abusive husband and a worsening drinking problem. Paula recalls her contented childhood, the audacity she learned as a teenager, the exhilaration of her romance with Charlo, and the marriage to him that left her feeling powerless.   <span id="freeText10379968742217453048" style="display:none">Paula Spencer is a thirty-nine-year-old working-class woman struggling to reclaim her dignity after marriage to an abusive husband and a worsening drinking problem. Paula recalls her contented childhood, the audacity she learned as a teenager, the exhilaration of her romance with Charlo, and the marriage to him that left her feeling powerless. Capturing both her vulnerability and her strength, Roddy Doyle gives Paula a voice that is real and unforgettable.</span>

<div class='communityQuestion'>
<div class='questionText'>
<a href="/questions/1450882-what-was-the-relationship-issue">what was the relationship ( issue / problem ) inferred to by Carmel with her father?</a>
</div>
<div class='communityQAFooter questionFooter'>
<ul class='questionFooterActions'>
<li class='footerItem like'>
<span class='likeItContainer' id='like_container_community_question_1450882'><a rel="nofollow" class="jsLike like_it" href="https://www.goodreads.com/user/new?remember=true&amp;return_url=%2Fbook%2Fshow%2F52778.The_Woman_Who_Walked_Into_Doors">like</a></span>

</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionTimestamp'>
<a href="/questions/1450882-what-was-the-relationship-issue">2 years ago</a>
</li>
<li class='footerItem communityQuestionPermalink'>
<a href="/questions/1450882-what-was-the-relationship-issue">Add your answer</a>
</li>

</div>
<div class='allCommunityAnswers'>
<div class='communityAnswer'>
<div class='communityAnswerBody'>
<div class='answererUserIcon'>
<a class="leftAlignedImage userPhoto" href="/user/show/9247460-maddison"><img alt="" src="https://i.gr-assets.com/images/S/compressed.photo.goodreads.com/users/1496646613i/9247460._UX100_CR0,0,100,100_.jpg" /></a>
</div>
<div class='communityAnswerTextContainer'>
<span class='communityAnswererName'>
<a href="/user/show/9247460-maddison">Maddison</a>
</span>
<span class='communityAnswerText'>
<div class='spoiler'>
This answer contains spoilers…
<span class='spoilerAnswerText'>
<a class="jsShowSpoiler spoilerAction">(view spoiler)</a><span class="spoilerContainer" style="display: none">[
  
    I believe we're supposed to infer that the father was abusive in some form. Paula, even if she were abused by her father, may not have realized it was<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">…more</a>
    I believe we're supposed to infer that the father was abusive in some form. Paula, even if she were abused by her father, may not have realized it was abuse, or maybe had blamed herself for his inappropriate behavior - just like she does with Charlo later in the story. Even when she is mistreated by men, she looks back on the occasions as "not that bad" or "her fault for instigating it" or "something he didn't really mean," and so we can assume she also viewed her father this way. Carmel, on the other hand, is written as a much more realistic - albeit cynical - character, who may view the father's abuse as exactly that: abuse. Even into adulthood, Carmel was the one who saw the way Charlo abused Paula and offered her an out. Her reluctance to talk about her father's abuse towards her might stem from either too much hurt to want to bring it up/relive the memory or she realizes that no matter what she says, Paula will always convince herself that abusive men shouldn't take the blame for their abuse (or, perhaps a combination of the two). I hope that helped!<a href="#" rel="nofollow noopener">(less)</a>
  
 <a class="jsHideSpoiler spoilerAction">(hide spoiler)</a>]</span>
</span>
</div>
</span>
</div>

    <div class="section firstReview">
      

<div id="review_1469501635" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1469501635" />
    <a title="Dannii Elle" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle"><img alt="Dannii Elle" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1508499419p2/44487975.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1469501635?book_show_action=true">Oct 22, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Dannii Elle" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Dannii Elle" href="/user/show/44487975-dannii-elle">Dannii Elle</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860219825" href="/book/show/979003.The_Woman_Who_Walked_Into_Doors">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/44487975-dannii-elle?shelf=literary-fiction-love">literary-fiction-love</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1469501635" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4094063965160503030">This author has been on my radar since a creative course in university, when my lecturer provided us with her self-curated list of 100 books/authors to read in our lifetime. Roddy Doyle's name headed the piece. I acquired a collection of his best known works and then did nothing else with them for a few years.<br/><br/>I admit I was fearful that his writing might not have aged well, when I read the synopsis and reviews that spoke of this cultural focus. I was wrong. So very wrong. Doyle provides a startli</span>
  <span id="freeText4094063965160503030" style="display:none">This author has been on my radar since a creative course in university, when my lecturer provided us with her self-curated list of 100 books/authors to read in our lifetime. Roddy Doyle's name headed the piece. I acquired a collection of his best known works and then did nothing else with them for a few years.<br /><br />I admit I was fearful that his writing might not have aged well, when I read the synopsis and reviews that spoke of this cultural focus. I was wrong. So very wrong. Doyle provides a startling and insightful vision of suburban Irish dialogue and society. His characters felt authentic and their flaws exuded life and vitality often missing from other contemporary literature, I have read. The dialogue also radiated personality. Each character was given a voice truly of their own and the nuances in tone and expression made them immediately recognisable, to the reader.<br /><br />This was also a devastatingly sad read. As protagonist, Paula, takes us through her life in a series of flashbacks, we are invited to bear witness to the utter sorrow that has chronicled it. This entire piece was raw emotion. In places it was almost <i>too</i> sorrowful to continue reading and I spent much of my reading straining to make sense of the words, through tear-filled and red-rimmed eyes. There is a brutality evident in every scene this delivers.<br /><br />This emotion is what made this an unforgettable piece. It has an important story to tell and a powerful message to deliver. It is also one I don't feel I could ever deliver, for how deeply its words have wounded it, despite the five stars I have awarded it. It is not a piece without hope but the horror ultimately outweighed the ending, for me.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4094063965160503030" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_587531350" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/587531350" />
    <a title="Tony" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/33771-tony"><img alt="Tony" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1292869016p2/33771.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/587531350?book_show_action=true">Apr 11, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Tony" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Tony" href="/user/show/33771-tony">Tony</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was ok"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was ok</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Hardcover 9780670867752" href="/book/show/381711.The_Woman_Who_Walked_Into_Doors">review of another edition</a>


        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/33771-tony?shelf=irish">irish</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer587531350" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer11838248056807937467">I have given The Speech at least a hundred times. At least. The setting, the words, and sadly the result are essentially the same.<br/><br/>There is bustle all about. But I find the quietest room available. It’s me. And Her. And a cop or a counselor. <br/><br/>It’s not always the same HER, of course. But some are repeaters. Those ones have heard The Speech before, but they act as if they haven’t.<br/><br/>She’s been beaten. A little or a lot. Enough to call the cops. And now here we are, three to seven days later. She wants </span>
  <span id="freeText11838248056807937467" style="display:none">I have given The Speech at least a hundred times. At least. The setting, the words, and sadly the result are essentially the same.<br /><br />There is bustle all about. But I find the quietest room available. It’s me. And Her. And a cop or a counselor. <br /><br />It’s not always the same HER, of course. But some are repeaters. Those ones have heard The Speech before, but they act as if they haven’t.<br /><br />She’s been beaten. A little or a lot. Enough to call the cops. And now here we are, three to seven days later. She wants him out, which I know means She will take him back. The Speech is explanatory, detailed; some of it is ‘cover my ass’. When The Speech is over it will be my call, not Her’s. Yet almost every time I will do what She wants. It’s just first I have to give The Speech. She will want him out because of ‘the kids’. Which is why, near the end of The Speech, I make sure I tell Her that these things are generational: that if her son sees this, he will grow up to hit; and if her daughter sees this she will grow up and let herself be beaten. It is at that moment that I see a flicker of understanding. But it doesn’t change the resolve. I have not changed Her mind. It is then I think, and sometimes say, that the next beaten women who call the cops and follows through will be the first. That sadness sometimes gets a flicker too, but nothing more.<br /><br />Paula Spencer, in <i>The Women Who Walked into Doors</i>, is such a woman. I read it because I read Roddy Doyle’s <i>Two Pints</i> and thought it was hilarious. Not great literature, mind you; but Doyle clearly has an ear. For the language of Dublinese. He has an ‘ear’ here too, in this otherwise depressing tale. But there are no insights and no great writing. It’s as if, fresh off his Booker, he decided to write a novel about domestic violence. <br /><br />Told in the first person, there are rambling monologues by Paula. She repeats herself, the same phrases over and over – ‘Leave my Mammy alone’ ‘I fell’ ‘No one saw me’. She calls herself ‘The woman who walked into walls’ four times in four pages. <br /><br /><i>He loved me and he beat me. I loved him and I took it. It’s as simple as that</i>.<br /><br />No, it can’t be. It’s hard for me to understand because I’ve never had the urge. But I know it’s not that simple. <br /><br />This book did nothing to help my understanding or my sadness. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="11838248056807937467" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_18452461" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18452461" />
    <a title="will" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1015266-will"><img alt="will" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1345397159p2/1015266.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18452461?book_show_action=true">Mar 23, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="will" class="user" itemprop="url" name="will" href="/user/show/1015266-will">will</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18452461" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2640254189210185185"><br/><br/>The Woman Who Walked Into Doors by Roddy Doyle.<br/><br/>Roddy Doyle is a wonderful writer. Normally his books are fast reads, he writes is an easy going flowing way. His books contain a certain amount of humour but that is because he writes "slice of life" stories. His characters are real, the stories are real and real life (or so I've been told) contains a certain amount of humour.<br/><br/>The Woman Who walked Into Doors possesses many of these ingredients but there is a shadow over the book. It is a love story </span>
  <span id="freeText2640254189210185185" style="display:none"><br /><br />The Woman Who Walked Into Doors by Roddy Doyle.<br /><br />Roddy Doyle is a wonderful writer. Normally his books are fast reads, he writes is an easy going flowing way. His books contain a certain amount of humour but that is because he writes "slice of life" stories. His characters are real, the stories are real and real life (or so I've been told) contains a certain amount of humour.<br /><br />The Woman Who walked Into Doors possesses many of these ingredients but there is a shadow over the book. It is a love story but it is the story of a victim, a woman who is beaten by her husband, a woman who spends time in hospital, hiding the beatings with a simple excuse: I walked into a door. Whereas normally Doyle's stories flow, this shadow makes the book a slow read. It is a thin book, a mere 200+ pages, but it is a very thick read. The end of the book contains so much violence, violence of language, violence of action, violence of deeds. It is a redeeming story, Paula Spencer finally escapes from her prison - instead of walking into doors she finds the key to open the door and leave. But the story is so harrowing that there is no sense of relief at the end. I finished the book drained and upset.<br /><br />Doyle has written a follow up, called Paula Spencer, that sits on my bookshelf. I suppose I should read it straight away. There has to be a happy ending (or at least I hope there is) but at this moment I don't really want to drag myself back into Paula's world. I need some light relief.<br /><br />This was a good book, a not an enjoyable book, because the story isn't one that you could really "enjoy". It tells a tale that, by the end has an outcome where all of the victims of Charlo's violence are still alive - if they actually have lives, remains to be seen.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="2640254189210185185" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer14167083676557372363">I decided to re-read this before reading the sequel <a href='https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30510.Paula_Spencer' title='Paula Spencer by Roddy Doyle' rel='nofollow noopener'>Paula Spencer</a>. I had forgotten just how good this book is, just how well Doyle does a female protagonist. The book is painful and sad and unflinching in it's descriptions of marital abuse and alcoholism but as always, Doyle adds in warmth and humor to make it all hurt less. <br/><br/>After my re-read I'm not sure that I want to read the sequel. I don't want to ruin the image in my head with a new story that might not be as good. Plus on the jacket it say</span>
  <span id="freeText14167083676557372363" style="display:none">I decided to re-read this before reading the sequel <a href="https://www.goodreads.com/book/show/30510.Paula_Spencer" title="Paula Spencer by Roddy Doyle" rel="nofollow noopener">Paula Spencer</a>. I had forgotten just how good this book is, just how well Doyle does a female protagonist. The book is painful and sad and unflinching in it's descriptions of marital abuse and alcoholism but as always, Doyle adds in warmth and humor to make it all hurt less. <br /><br />After my re-read I'm not sure that I want to read the sequel. I don't want to ruin the image in my head with a new story that might not be as good. Plus on the jacket it says that Paula finds a new man in the new book-I don't know that I can bear it if the story becomes a cutesy love story.<br /><br />Roddy Doyle tells the story of Paula Spencer, a woman in an abusive marraige who has developed a drinking problem herself. Through flashbacks we learn about Paula's adolescence and courtship with her husband and we learn about the devastating abuse she suffered at his hands. The book is heartbreaking and gut-wrenching and so well done. </span>

<div id="review_18902681" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/18902681" />
    <a title="Danna" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/637121-danna"><img alt="Danna" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1469202464p2/637121.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/18902681?book_show_action=true">Mar 29, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Danna" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Danna" href="/user/show/637121-danna">Danna</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer18902681" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer2037776756465643523">I picked this one up from a display at the library. I had skimmed a few pages and the writing style caught my eye (he uses punctuation and italics to visually illustrate dialog and flashbacks). I think the author did a great job telling the sadly-classic story of the abused woman, how that situation came to be and the culture in which the situation flourished, how she finally found the strength to kick her husband out of the house and keep on living. I liked the way he was able to explore how su</span>
  <span id="freeText2037776756465643523" style="display:none">I picked this one up from a display at the library. I had skimmed a few pages and the writing style caught my eye (he uses punctuation and italics to visually illustrate dialog and flashbacks). I think the author did a great job telling the sadly-classic story of the abused woman, how that situation came to be and the culture in which the situation flourished, how she finally found the strength to kick her husband out of the house and keep on living. I liked the way he was able to explore how subjective our memories really are, particularly the way we can re-write our own histories in our minds as a coping mechanism until we're ready to (or forced to) face the facts more clearly. Because we all see, interpret, and respond to the world differently, the glimpses into the sisters' views of their family experiences add interesting layers to the storytelling. Some reviews I read expressed disappointment in what was thought thought to be a depressing and/or unsatisfying ending without closure, but I thought it was honest and complete. I hear there's a sequel - I have many other books on my to-read list that I'm more excited about, so I doubt I'll get around to looking for it. I was satisfied with the ending of this one.</span>
  <a data-text-id="2037776756465643523" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2831643764" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2831643764" />
    <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads"><img alt="Abbie | ab_reads" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1545340790p2/53055819.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2831643764?book_show_action=true">May 25, 2019</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Abbie | ab_reads" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Abbie | ab_reads" href="/user/show/53055819-abbie-ab-reads">Abbie | ab_reads</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9781860219825" href="/book/show/979003.The_Woman_Who_Walked_Into_Doors">review of another edition</a>


        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2831643764" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12079788014123265184">I’d never have expected what I got in this book just from the cover and title, but once you actually take a second to think about it, think about what the old excuse of ‘Oh I walked into the door’ to cover up a bruise or black eye actually means, then you’ll begin to have an inkling of what this book entails.<br/>.<br/>As I mentioned when I started reading it, I was hesitant to find a male author writing a female protagonist, as we’ve all encountered those male-authored women who think of nothing else but</span>
  <span id="freeText12079788014123265184" style="display:none">I’d never have expected what I got in this book just from the cover and title, but once you actually take a second to think about it, think about what the old excuse of ‘Oh I walked into the door’ to cover up a bruise or black eye actually means, then you’ll begin to have an inkling of what this book entails.<br />.<br />As I mentioned when I started reading it, I was hesitant to find a male author writing a female protagonist, as we’ve all encountered those male-authored women who think of nothing else but the way their nipples feel under their shirts all day long. I’m happy to tell you however, that Roddy Doyle is fully capable of having Paula go through the day without thinking unnecessary sexual thoughts about her OWN SELF. I know that’s a low bar, but thankfully he also goes above and beyond that, and I really enjoyed her sarcastic, humorous, and tragically beaten down narrative voice.<br />.<br />It’s set in Dublin and focuses on a working class family, as many of Doyle’s books do apparently, and truly Paula’s story just broke my heart. From her childhood where she was forced to grow up quickly because of the people in her life - from her friends, her vile desk mate, and mediocre to downright perverted teachers, I think Doyle paints an accurate if grim picture of working-class families in Dublin in the 60s and 70s.<br />.<br />And it sadly doesn’t get any better for Paula. She becomes trapped in a violent marriage, and Doyle does an amazing job of detailing her thought processes both during the marriage and 17 years later when she’s reflecting back. His style works really well for this, with a sprinkle of humour and Paula’s rough mouth adding some lightness to the dark. For some reason though, my edition was littered with random bits of punctuation that I’m sure should not have been there. Like a • in the middle of a word or I,m instead of I’m, and it was really quite off-putting. Thankfully they stopped about two thirds of the way through.<br />.<br />It is a very triggering book so proceed with caution if you’re sensitive to all types of assault and abuse. I also recommend going to read @aseriesofunreadbooks review because Lauren is far more eloquent than me!<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="12079788014123265184" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_650156602" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/650156602" />
    <a title="Kiessa" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5826468-kiessa"><img alt="Kiessa" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1330412051p2/5826468.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/650156602?book_show_action=true">Jun 24, 2013</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Kiessa" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Kiessa" href="/user/show/5826468-kiessa">Kiessa</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="did not like it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">did not like it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/5826468-kiessa?shelf=gave-up-on">gave-up-on</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer650156602" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer83606420091192548">First, I'll admit that I am currently on page 79 of 226. If I had to rate my desire to keep reading from one to ten, ten being the most compelled to go on, I'd have to say that I'm about at -57.<br/><br/>Next, let me get this out of the way. I'm no prude, and I occasionally enjoy cursing like a sailor. But even I was shocked by Mr. Doyle's overuse of the words f* and c&*#. So much so, in fact, that I can't bring myself to retype the words because I'm so over-exposed to them. The volume of cursing was a di</span>
  <span id="freeText83606420091192548" style="display:none">First, I'll admit that I am currently on page 79 of 226. If I had to rate my desire to keep reading from one to ten, ten being the most compelled to go on, I'd have to say that I'm about at -57.<br /><br />Next, let me get this out of the way. I'm no prude, and I occasionally enjoy cursing like a sailor. But even I was shocked by Mr. Doyle's overuse of the words f* and c&amp;*#. So much so, in fact, that I can't bring myself to retype the words because I'm so over-exposed to them. The volume of cursing was a distracting and unnecessary turn-off.<br /><br />So Mr. Doyle has written a book about domestic violence. This is neither a surprise nor a spoiler; it was referenced on the back of the book. I have read other books about domestic violence, fiction and non-fiction, and have worked with survivors of domestic and sexual assault. Perhaps this is why I find this book to be so desperately inadequate.<br /><br />Over 79 repetitive pages (filled with uncreative cursing, I repeat), this author has reduced the central character to one role. That of victim. Do we as readers really believe other human beings can be so completely uni-dimensional and incapable of other defining personality traits beyond their subjugation to others? Is it possible that a woman, any woman, could ONLY be defined by the long string of men who have abused her, everyone from her classmates to the milkman to her teachers to her husband? I personally don't think so. Nowhere in these 79 pages does there seem to be any semblance of a well-developed character.<br /><br />There are so many books that capture the complexity of domestic violence with brilliant writing, rich characters, and a depth of understanding. Unfortunately, I don't think this novel is one of them. Anyone seeking an alternative that feels real and true might find something that will stick to their ribs in Here on Earth by Alice Hoffmann. <br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="83606420091192548" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1174983999" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1174983999" />
    <a title="Lyn Elliott" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/10312986-lyn-elliott"><img alt="Lyn Elliott" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1377589438p2/10312986.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1174983999?book_show_action=true">Jan 20, 2015</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Lyn Elliott" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Lyn Elliott" href="/user/show/10312986-lyn-elliott">Lyn Elliott</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Lyn by: </span>
      Book club
  </div>


          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=fiction">fiction</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=women">women</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/10312986-lyn-elliott?shelf=book-club">book-club</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1174983999" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4024602777131317435">I came to this book reluctantly. Another book club choice I hadn't made; didn't want to read about domestic violence in general or an abused woman in particular.<br/><br/>But Roddy Doyle hooked me from the unexpected start, 'I was told by a Guard who came to the door. He wasn't one I'd seen before, one of the usual ones....I knew before he spoke. It clicked inside me when I opened the door. (For years opening that door scared the life out of me. I hated it; it terrified me)'.<br/><br/>And straight away we are into </span>
  <span id="freeText4024602777131317435" style="display:none">I came to this book reluctantly. Another book club choice I hadn't made; didn't want to read about domestic violence in general or an abused woman in particular.<br /><br />But Roddy Doyle hooked me from the unexpected start, 'I was told by a Guard who came to the door. He wasn't one I'd seen before, one of the usual ones....I knew before he spoke. It clicked inside me when I opened the door. (For years opening that door scared the life out of me. I hated it; it terrified me)'.<br /><br />And straight away we are into the chaos of Paula Spencer's life, the news that her violent husband Charlo is dead, wild kids, fear. Then a flash to when Paula and Charlo meet, and their immediate intense sexual attraction to each other. Another flash to Paula on the floor, Charlo standing over her. '"You fell, he said"'. No she hadn't. He had knocked her down. <br /><br />Then a rapid fire introduction to Paula's family, the O'Leary's. Most sections are very short. Like flashes of memory. Some incidents just a picture, sometimes whole conversations recalled in detail as Paula and her sisters talk together. Gradually something like a full story emerges from the pieces Paula remembers as she tries to piece her life together again, a year after she finally threw the by-now monstrous Charlo out of the house. She tries to recreate a good life, but there was no good life with Charlo, not after the first exhilaration had passed. First excitement. then excitement and fear together. Then just terror. <br /><br />The acts of violence don't occupy much space in the book and Paula doesn't face up to the fundamental questions until near the end. These come down to: 1) Why didn't anybody in the hospitals she went to so many times ask her the questions that would have allowed her to tell the truth - she hadn't dislocated her arm or broken her jaw by falling down the stairs (again) or had a black eye because she walked into a door. The husband who accompanied her to hospital had done it. But nobody ever did.<br />The doctors she saw never looked at her properly. They never looked her in the eye, never saw the whole of her. They smelt drink on her breath and that was that. <br /><br />2 'Why did he do it? He loved me and he beat me. I loved him and i took it. ... You can't love someone one minute, then beat them, and then love them again once the blood has been washed off. I can't separate the two things, the love and the beatings. ... I can't make two Charlos . I can't separate him into the good and the bad. I take the good and the bad comes too'. <br /><br />Doyle has created a believable battered woman, driven to alcohol as a means of survival, grappling to understand her life. Somehow she remains strong, despite the black despair she lived in for so long.<br />He has managed to speak in her voice throughout the book - difficult enough for a woman who has not known these desperate places herself, but quite extraordinary for a man. One of the reviewers quoted not eh back cover of the book clearly did not understand the hideous world of abuse and terror that Paula inhabited with Charlo. He (I presume it was a he) talked about 'the vulnerability and courage of a woman trapped in a loveless marriage'. Loveless marriage????? Come on. This was vicious, repeated criminal violence, carried out by a brutal man on the woman he professed to love, and she kept on loving him. Let's be honest and say a violent marriage.<br /><br />Doyle wrote this book about twenty years ago (first published 1996). The violence he wrote about then still exists, in all classes of society. Once can only hope that more women get the help they need than Paula and other beaten women did then, that more doctors and nurses see the whole person, and the police become more capable of dealing with domestic violence as criminal assault.<br /><br /> I want to read more Doyle to see what else he has to say about desperate lives, and to read about him so I can understand more about what drives him as a writer. <br /><br />'Paula Spencer' is a more recent sequel, set ten years later, when Paula has survived and has begun to stop drinking. I will read this. I won't however, go back to find the tv series of the Spencer Family which preceded 'The Woman who Walked into Doors'. The Spencers are so appalling, it is hard enough to confront them on the page when you can look away, go and do something else, not have to visualise too closely.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4024602777131317435" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_246489252" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/246489252" />
    <a title="Emilia P" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/76042-emilia-p"><img alt="Emilia P" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1417628630p2/76042.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/246489252?book_show_action=true">Dec 13, 2011</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Emilia P" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Emilia P" href="/user/show/76042-emilia-p">Emilia P</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/76042-emilia-p?shelf=real-books">real-books</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer246489252" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer15277735746640294593">This was a damn good book.<br/>Maybe "good" isn't the right word for it, but, well, Roddy D. was spot-on at getting a regular woman's voice to come through, filled with the uncompartmentalized joy, memory, despair, need, and hope that come with a hard life. <br/><br/>The first-person narrative flashes between the past -- a not altogether unpleasant youth, and a pretty dismal but relieved present wherein Paula Spencer has kicked her husband out of the house, only to find, a year later, that he's killed a woman </span>
  <span id="freeText15277735746640294593" style="display:none">This was a damn good book.<br />Maybe "good" isn't the right word for it, but, well, Roddy D. was spot-on at getting a regular woman's voice to come through, filled with the uncompartmentalized joy, memory, despair, need, and hope that come with a hard life. <br /><br />The first-person narrative flashes between the past -- a not altogether unpleasant youth, and a pretty dismal but relieved present wherein Paula Spencer has kicked her husband out of the house, only to find, a year later, that he's killed a woman and in turn been killed by the police. The chapters on the past inch ever closer and closer to the present -- the courtship, the wedding, the honeymoon, the first flat, the first beating. The interspersed chapters on the present reflect on her children as they are now, her semi-functional and desperately sad (but also, understandable!) alcoholism, and the facts of her husband's death. Finally, in a great and nearly unbearable torrent, she recounts the nearly two decades of marriage that are a blur, "mush", of beatings, bone breakings, being a lump on the floor, two black eyes, and how nobody saw, nobody asked, presumed she was a drunk and it was her own fault. Everyone (doctors, cashiers, etc) , not just her husband, saw her as a collection of parts, rather than as a whole person, who needed help. It was really, chilling<br /><br />What was so striking about this book to me was not really that terrible litany of pain, but the way she was brave enough to see it within the context of her larger life, and that she was able to claim her larger life at all. I hate to pull the "Irish voice" card, but the style here was a lot like the Patrick McCabe tone, a believable and relatable, sympathetic sort of madness which we all walk around with in our heads everyday. The messiness of one's interior life which doesn't, maybe can't, translate to public life.<br /><br />Roddy Doyle's pretty freaking great, and easy to read to boot. Thank you sir.</span>
  <a data-text-id="15277735746640294593" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_960392232" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/960392232" />
    <a title="Jo Davies" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/12974212-jo-davies"><img alt="Jo Davies" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1348080097p2/12974212.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/960392232?book_show_action=true">Jun 07, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Jo Davies" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Jo Davies" href="/user/show/12974212-jo-davies">Jo Davies</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>

          <span class="greyText">&nbsp;&middot;&nbsp;</span>
          <a class="lightGreyText" title="Paperback 9780099507741" href="/book/show/21038197-the-woman-who-walked-into-doors">review of another edition</a>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer960392232" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12185791035498109620">The fact that Roddy Doyle could write a book about a woman stuck in an abusive relationship and make it so utterly believable is a testament to his imagination and extreme skill as a story-teller. The story opens with Paula Spencer, a middle-aged Irish wife and mother, being told that her abusive husband Charlo has been killed by the police in an aborted attempt at kidnapping a local bank manager. This revelation fuels a boatload worth of memories of her marriage to the man at whose hands she su</span>
  <span id="freeText12185791035498109620" style="display:none">The fact that Roddy Doyle could write a book about a woman stuck in an abusive relationship and make it so utterly believable is a testament to his imagination and extreme skill as a story-teller. The story opens with Paula Spencer, a middle-aged Irish wife and mother, being told that her abusive husband Charlo has been killed by the police in an aborted attempt at kidnapping a local bank manager. This revelation fuels a boatload worth of memories of her marriage to the man at whose hands she suffered nearly two decades of humiliation and abuse. <br /><br />Doyle effectively uses a stream-of-consciousness style to make Paula's struggles breathtakingly immediate. You can almost feel the kicks, the slaps, the punches. You understand why Paula turns to alcohol to endure, as well as the motherly devotion which drags her back from oblivion. Doyle takes Paula's psyche and turns it inside out. He examines the guilt she feels for so many reasons: she's failed her children, she's disappointed her family, she has (nearly) allowed her husband to destroy her. <br /><br />Doyle asks the difficult questions about why women stay in abusive relationships and lets Paula answer them, with no punches pulled. <br /><br />Stunningly good.<br /> </span>
  <a data-text-id="12185791035498109620" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<span id="freeTextContainer12262944442257393657">Very vibrant rendering of the interior landscape of a working class woman in Dublin in the 90�s. From the perspective of age 39, Paula tells her current story, working on a poverty income as a cleaning woman, raising three children, and recovering from the death of her estranged husband, while constantly reflecting on memories that chart her progression from childhood. With much empathy and humor, Doyle does an outstanding job of portraying how she taps the well of energies, budding sexuality, a</span>
  <span id="freeText12262944442257393657" style="display:none">Very vibrant rendering of the interior landscape of a working class woman in Dublin in the 90�s. From the perspective of age 39, Paula tells her current story, working on a poverty income as a cleaning woman, raising three children, and recovering from the death of her estranged husband, while constantly reflecting on memories that chart her progression from childhood. With much empathy and humor, Doyle does an outstanding job of portraying how she taps the well of energies, budding sexuality, and liberating independence from her adolescence. The remembered dialog with friends and her large family from her youth is often exhilarating, something Doyle always excels at. The inaccuracy of memories is a bit of a theme in her ponderings, which especially comes up when her hopes to cherish loving relations are clouded or contradicted by abuse of one form or another or actions impaired by alcohol. Though the title of the book, and many sketches or blurbs about the book, might indicate that the novel is �about domestic violence�, the dark parts of the tale do not overwhelm the portrayal of the fervent life and soul of Paula.</span>

<div id="review_637139" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/637139" />
    <a title="Darryl" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/41249-darryl"><img alt="Darryl" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1175057161p2/41249.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/637139?book_show_action=true">Apr 08, 2007</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Darryl" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Darryl" href="/user/show/41249-darryl">Darryl</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">writers and survivors</span></div>

        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer637139" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer8719135558477691071">I'm off to Ireland in a month on a working vacation. Wanted to read some contemporay literature from the region and found this book of Doyle's. I liked it. I do work in theodicy (the problem of evil) and trauma theory, and so am always seeking after such at the level of somatic description. In this case, I found it in the consummate craft of Doyle's characterization of Paula. Doyle has a remarkable gift of habitation. Paula is a model of sustained, air-tight, character emanicipation and density.</span>
  <span id="freeText8719135558477691071" style="display:none">I'm off to Ireland in a month on a working vacation. Wanted to read some contemporay literature from the region and found this book of Doyle's. I liked it. I do work in theodicy (the problem of evil) and trauma theory, and so am always seeking after such at the level of somatic description. In this case, I found it in the consummate craft of Doyle's characterization of Paula. Doyle has a remarkable gift of habitation. Paula is a model of sustained, air-tight, character emanicipation and density.<br /><br />Finishing it, it really seems odd to attribute her experience of abuse to Doyle and his imagination. There is no sense of authorial spectre here, no compositional aftertaste. Just the story. In this respect, the book is a wonder. It is rare to find a persona so engaging and yet so autonomously rendered as she is. The novel has the added bonus of slowly and delicately breaking your heart, especially in Paula's observational description of another character's final, pathetic fate. Recommended for characterization.</span>
  <a data-text-id="8719135558477691071" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_1097882006" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1097882006" />
    <a title="Ashley" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/5042841-ashley"><img alt="Ashley" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1379099420p2/5042841.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/1097882006?book_show_action=true">Nov 04, 2014</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Ashley" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Ashley" href="/user/show/5042841-ashley">Ashley</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer1097882006" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer14217501732499843830">One of my major goals in the past few years has been to read more books by women, about women. I grew up reading books by men that purported to be for general audiences, but that all too often completely whiffed on the portrayal of women's interior lives (with "great" novels and "classic" authors either completely avoiding the issue, or relying heavily on tropes and stereotypes). Female characters written by women, on the other hand, typically ring truer, even when the character's life experienc</span>
  <span id="freeText14217501732499843830" style="display:none">One of my major goals in the past few years has been to read more books by women, about women. I grew up reading books by men that purported to be for general audiences, but that all too often completely whiffed on the portrayal of women's interior lives (with "great" novels and "classic" authors either completely avoiding the issue, or relying heavily on tropes and stereotypes). Female characters written by women, on the other hand, typically ring truer, even when the character's life experience and personality diverges significantly from my own and/or the book is otherwise terrible. I suppose there's a reason writers are often advised to write what they know.<br /><br />So when I say that I read this book and assumed, until I looked up the author's biography, that it must have been written by a woman, you can understand that to be a compliment of the highest order, both personal and professional. Roddy Doyle's empathy for and insight into Paula Spencer's mental anguish is extraordinary. Such a good book; such a fantastic author. Will definitely be checking out more of his work. </span>
  <a data-text-id="14217501732499843830" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2017645003" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2017645003" />
    <a title="Guy" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/3163796-guy"><img alt="Guy" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1286648000p2/3163796.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2017645003?book_show_action=true">Jun 10, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Guy" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Guy" href="/user/show/3163796-guy">Guy</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="really liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">really liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

          <div class="uitext greyText bookshelves">
            Shelves:
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3163796-guy?shelf=ireland">ireland</a>, 
              <a class="actionLinkLite" href="/review/list/3163796-guy?shelf=2017">2017</a>
          </div>
    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2017645003" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer4574426299448430139">The protagonist of Roddy Doyle’s 1996 novel, The Woman Who Walked Into Doors is 39-year-old Dublin mother of four, Paula Spencer. When the novel opens, a Guard arrives at Paula’s door. This is not an unusual event as the police frequently come knocking at the door looking for Charlo, a man with a criminal past, but this time is different…<br/><br/>From that moment, Paula recalls her story of life with Charlo, how they met, their torrid courtship, her father’s strong disapproval, and the highlight of Paula</span>
  <span id="freeText4574426299448430139" style="display:none">The protagonist of Roddy Doyle’s 1996 novel, The Woman Who Walked Into Doors is 39-year-old Dublin mother of four, Paula Spencer. When the novel opens, a Guard arrives at Paula’s door. This is not an unusual event as the police frequently come knocking at the door looking for Charlo, a man with a criminal past, but this time is different…<br /><br />From that moment, Paula recalls her story of life with Charlo, how they met, their torrid courtship, her father’s strong disapproval, and the highlight of Paula and Charlo’s life together: the wedding. From here, things go downhill: the novel includes some flashback details of domestic abuse.<br /><br />The novel goes back and forth from the present to the past as Paula recalls her marriage. In the present, Paula, an alcoholic (and we gradually learn how that happened) is a cleaner. She cleans a bank in the early evenings, and during the day, she cleans the houses of women her age who are considerably better off.<br /><br />"I like seeing into other people’s houses. Funny, I hardly ever feel jealous. And I should, because some of the houses are incredible. Huge. Some of the stuff in them, I wouldn’t want most of it myself but it must have cost a fortune. Dark furniture, flat-screened tellies, CD players with tiny little speakers. I love music. There’s one house I do on Mondays, in Clontarf; they’ve a great collection of CDS, all the seventies stuff. I got her to show me how to use the CD player. There was no problem. I like her, the owner. Miriam. We’re the same age. We both went to the same dances when we were kids. I don’t remember her. She married a doctor. I married Charlo. "<br /><br />Paula’s story is intimate: she talks to us of her adolescence, burgeoning sexuality (you were either a “slut or a tight bitch,”) her harmless married fantasy life (at one point, she had a crush on a bus conductor), her relationships with her family, Charlo’s intimidating family, and her children. All through these memories, Charlo appears, almost as though he enters and exits the door, looking for his meals, his clean, ironed clothes and someone to absorb his violence. Author Roddy Doyle convincingly shows Paula’s reluctance to admit how bad her marriage became, how she lost an entire decade somehow.<br /><br />Paula tells her story with vibrancy, tenacity, and intense humanity. There’s also the sense that it’s an underground voice, swelling from behind closed doors, and emergency room visits that hide the true nature of her injuries. She meets other women shepherded in to the ER by their supposedly caring, concerned husbands. Yes the number of ‘clumsy’ women at the emergency room are legion. No one asks awkward questions, no one looks directly into the eyes of the victims, but everyone goes along with the stories that these women have fallen down the stairs or, as the title states, ‘walked into doors.’<br /><br />A word on style. I read some reviews complaining about the author’s style. This was very readable, but without quotation marks if that bothers anyone. The sentences are sometimes very short as they mirror speech, and Paula is speaking to us here, so sometimes she corrects or expands her thoughts with one word. The domestic abuse is recalled with a surreal quality that echoes the rapidity and illogical circumstances of Charlo’s violent rages. So in other words, it’s not blow-by-blow but rather the violence is impressionistic.<br /><br />Of course, we all cheer for Paula, a likeable woman who feels very real and who’s survived adversity with the scars to prove it.</span>
  <a data-text-id="4574426299448430139" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_36800308" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/36800308" />
    <a title="Drgibson63" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/1366225-drgibson63"><img alt="Drgibson63" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/36800308?book_show_action=true">Nov 02, 2008</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Drgibson63" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Drgibson63" href="/user/show/1366225-drgibson63">Drgibson63</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>


          <div><span class="uitext greyText">Recommends it for: </span>
           <span class="uitext reviewText">Any adult</span></div>

          <div>
    <span class="greyText">Recommended to Drgibson63 by: </span>
      Friend
  </div>


    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
                  <em>
                    This review has been hidden because it contains spoilers. To view it,
                    <a href="#" onclick="Effect.toggle(&#39;reviewTextContainer36800308&#39;); return false;">click here.</a>
                  </em>
              <span id="reviewTextContainer36800308" class="readable"
                    style="display:none"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer649165820150441376">The Woman Who walked Into Doors<br/><br/>Irish novelist Roddy Doyle writes very well about family life. His previous novels, which include The Van, The Snapper and The Commitments, are insightful, funny peeks into the personal lives of families who argue a lot, but also love each other. A recent work, The Woman Who Walked Into Walls, is his best yet, but it may not be for all tastes. This book, told in first-person narrative, is a profoundly depressing story about wife beating, and the ripping apart of on</span>
  <span id="freeText649165820150441376" style="display:none">The Woman Who walked Into Doors<br /><br />Irish novelist Roddy Doyle writes very well about family life. His previous novels, which include The Van, The Snapper and The Commitments, are insightful, funny peeks into the personal lives of families who argue a lot, but also love each other. A recent work, The Woman Who Walked Into Walls, is his best yet, but it may not be for all tastes. This book, told in first-person narrative, is a profoundly depressing story about wife beating, and the ripping apart of one woman's self esteem by her sadistic, abusive husband. The scars don't completely heal, even after the monster has left the home.<br /><br />Paula Spencer is in her late 30s. Her husband Charlo, who she threw out of the house a year earlier, has just been killed by the police after kidnapping and killing a woman in her home. Doyle paints us a picture of Paula's current life. She's raising three kids, the youngest five, the oldest, a young woman named Nicola. Paula has a menial job cleaning homes and offices and is also an alcoholic, but doesn't drink until after the youngest is in bed. Although we know Paula was abused by her husband, that is not the main focus early on. At times the tone of the novel is almost light. Paula directs her household with a good-natured gung-ho attitude in the mornings. She argues with her sisters, especially Carmen, about their own family's past.<br /><br />Doyle offers readers two separate stories for more than half of the novel. One chapter deals with Paula's post-marriage life and trying to deal with Charlo's death. The next chapter recounts Paula's life as a teen and her eventual meeting with, courtship and marriage with Charlo, a good looking hood who had a prison record and a good job. He was a ÿride,ÿ in Paula's words, a man that every girl wanted, and he chose her for his wife.<br /><br />Charlo first hits Paula when she's pregnant with Nicola. Then he hits her again, and again, and again, and again, and then some more. In one very long extremely difficult-to-read chapter, we are provided a glimpse of the personal 17 years of hell that Paula experiences as a battered wife. As brutal as the abuse is -- it includes punching, kicking, breaking bones, using a fist to induce a miscarriage, yanking arms out of sockets, hair dragging and mental abuse -- the damage inflicted to her psyche and self esteem by her husband is as cruel. She becomes a cipher, a depressed, alcoholic, beaten in front of her children, who summons just enough energy to take care of her kids. She's whipped, yet still loves a husband who tortures her because she believes he puts up with such a worthless woman.<br /><br />"He butted me his head. ... He kicked me up and he kicked me down the stairs. Bruised me, scalded me, threatened me. For seventeen years. Hit me, thumped me, raped me. ... Fists, boots, knee, head. Bread knife, saucepan, brush. He tore out clumps of my hair. Cigarettes, lighter ashtray. ... Months went by and nothing happened, but it was always there -- the promise of it."<br /><br />Doyle brings out in detail another terror of being abused. The fact that nobody cares or wants to know. Paula is admitted to hospitals by Charlo for having "walked into a door" or "fallen down the stairs." She hopes someone will ask her what's wrong, fantasizes about telling the truth. But no doctor or nurse ever does. Her mother, her father, never express concern, despite the obvious deterioration of her personality.<br /><br />There is an irony to this, however. When sister Carmen returns from England, she sees what no one will speak of, and begs Paula to leave. But Paula can't, and in fact hates Carmen for her concern. Besides, Charlo has threatened to kill her if she ever leaves.<br /><br />Something finally causes Paula to kick Charlo out. It's a spontaneous act, fueled by a terrible evil. When Charlo is booted out, with the help of a large frying pan, Paula discovers her husband, like all abusers, is a coward. He leaves and stays gone.<br /><br />The Woman Who Walked Into Walls is similar in theme to Stephen King's Dolores Claiborne. But Doyle is more effective than King at showing the after-effects of long-term abuse. Paula Spencer, once a fun-loving pretty girl named Paula O;Leary who married and used to wait with joy for her new husband to come home, is still a shell of her former self. The beatings stopped long ago, and Paula proves herself a survivor. But refilling a self whittled to a hollow shell takes a long time.<br /><br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="649165820150441376" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>

<div id="review_2166910872" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/2166910872" />
    <a title="Victoria Wallin" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/73102490-victoria-wallin"><img alt="Victoria Wallin" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1604325039p2/73102490.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/2166910872?book_show_action=true">Oct 28, 2017</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Victoria Wallin" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Victoria Wallin" href="/user/show/73102490-victoria-wallin">Victoria Wallin</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="it was amazing"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">it was amazing</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span></span>

        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer2166910872" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer1789712307383951684">Even though I read this novel many years ago, this irish tale of Paula Spencer and her trying to survive domestic abuse has never left my mind. <br/><br/>Roddy Doyle's description of this small irish village and its working class people struggling to stay together and society's judgements is cleverly put together with a gritty, raw language that is beautiful in its ugly truths. It is kitchen sink realism in one of its better forms. <br/><br/>The shock of being hit the first time, the hope that it will never happen </span>
  <span id="freeText1789712307383951684" style="display:none">Even though I read this novel many years ago, this irish tale of Paula Spencer and her trying to survive domestic abuse has never left my mind. <br /><br />Roddy Doyle's description of this small irish village and its working class people struggling to stay together and society's judgements is cleverly put together with a gritty, raw language that is beautiful in its ugly truths. It is kitchen sink realism in one of its better forms. <br /><br />The shock of being hit the first time, the hope that it will never happen again, the love for the man, the lying, saying she was so clumsy she constantly walked into doors, all of these things are described to us by Paula, and it is impossible to judge her. It could be me, is what one thinks. What would I do? If anything, this book shows us that we probably can't answer that question until it actually happens to us. And God forbid that it ever would.<br /><br />This is one of those books that stays with you forever.<br /></span>
  <a data-text-id="1789712307383951684" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>

<div id="review_1217004929" class="review nosyndicate" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/1217004929" />
    <a title="KateMc" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/17386712-katemc"><img alt="KateMc" src="https://s.gr-assets.com/assets/nophoto/user/u_50x66-632230dc9882b4352d753eedf9396530.png" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">

<div id="review_3436554730" class="review" itemprop="reviews" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Review">
  <link itemprop="url" href="https://www.goodreads.com/review/show/3436554730" />
    <a title="Patrick Connolly" class="left imgcol" href="/user/show/118213500-patrick-connolly"><img alt="Patrick Connolly" src="https://images.gr-assets.com/users/1603360783p2/118213500.jpg" /></a>

  <div class="left bodycol">
    <div class="reviewHeader uitext stacked">
        <a class="reviewDate createdAt right" href="/review/show/3436554730?book_show_action=true">Oct 21, 2020</a>

      <span itemprop="author" itemscope itemtype="http://schema.org/Person">
        <a title="Patrick Connolly" class="user" itemprop="url" name="Patrick Connolly" href="/user/show/118213500-patrick-connolly">Patrick Connolly</a>
      </span>

        rated it
        <span class=" staticStars notranslate" title="liked it"><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10">liked it</span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p10"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span><span size="15x15" class="staticStar p0"></span></span>



        

    </div>


        <div class="reviewText stacked">
              <span id="reviewTextContainer3436554730" class="readable"
              >
            
<span id="freeTextContainer12883304385398130091">The books that I've read by Roddy Doyle have been brimming with an uncomfortable and embarrassing joy. This joy is replaced with pain in <i>The Woman Who Walked Into Doors</i>. <br/><br/>There are some obvious problematic aspects of this book. For one, a male author taking on a topic like domestic violence with a female narrator in first person is kind of a weird move. The loose, fragmented style seems to be stereotypical of women's writing like Virginia Woolf. I wouldn't say this is a feminist book, and I think</span>
  <span id="freeText12883304385398130091" style="display:none">The books that I've read by Roddy Doyle have been brimming with an uncomfortable and embarrassing joy. This joy is replaced with pain in <i>The Woman Who Walked Into Doors</i>. <br /><br />There are some obvious problematic aspects of this book. For one, a male author taking on a topic like domestic violence with a female narrator in first person is kind of a weird move. The loose, fragmented style seems to be stereotypical of women's writing like Virginia Woolf. I wouldn't say this is a feminist book, and I think it fails the Bechdel test. A lot of critics in the first few pages note this book for its compassion, but I feel like it's a little exploitative. <br /><br />Maybe for an Irish woman in the nineties (or now), this was the center of her universe: her husband, her kids, her parents, her in-laws, and there may be a lot of pressure coming from them to stay the course no matter how terrible it is. So this may be based in reality and a necessary story to be told from a writer who does a great job of making you care about average people, people who if you saw on the bus or in the supermarket, you probably wouldn't have wandering daydreams about their existence. <br /><br />The parts that were good were good. The wedding scene, the first time she meets Charlo, the first time she meets Charlo's family, the description of the robbery gone wrong. These scenes are charged and the details, the weird ones, felt alive. But these moments of detailed narrative don't come often enough, and overall, the structure of the book is too dreamy and confusing. The repetitive elements made me feel like I was in a clothesdryer, bumping back into the same image, sentence, or paragraph in a jarring way. I'm not sure if this is a fair criticism, but I thought a lot about the writer writing the book instead of getting lost in the world of the character.<br /><br />This is where I get confused. Does the subject matter of alcoholism and domestic violence contribute to this structure? Does the repetitive nature of the narration illustrate a destroyed mind playing tricks on itself? Or is this just a way to dance around, and sometimes on, touchy subjects that should be handled with care? It's unclear. And I think that's another way to describe elements of the book.<br /><br />One last thought, my edition of the book, not a first edition, had a lot of typos and punctuation errors. Especially because the narrator is poor, this felt in bad taste and I couldn't tell what was on purpose or not.<br /><br />I bought this book used for something like $5 at Mac's Backs on Coventry in Cleveland Heights. Mac's Backs is my favorite book store in the Cleveland area. </span>
  <a data-text-id="12883304385398130091" href="#" onclick="swapContent($(this));; return false;">...more</a>

          </span>
        </div>
